<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Cooking</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Preparing food using heat</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the preparation of food. For a general outline, see <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_food_preparation" title="Outline of food preparation">Outline of food preparation</a>. For varied styles of international food, see <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisine</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agdz-rosino-05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Agdz-rosino-05.jpg/220px-Agdz-rosino-05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Agdz-rosino-05.jpg/330px-Agdz-rosino-05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Agdz-rosino-05.jpg/440px-Agdz-rosino-05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agdz-rosino-05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A person cooking in a <a href="/wiki/Restaurant" title="Restaurant">restaurant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cooking_in_Villages.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Cooking_in_Villages.jpg/220px-Cooking_in_Villages.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Cooking_in_Villages.jpg/330px-Cooking_in_Villages.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Cooking_in_Villages.jpg/440px-Cooking_in_Villages.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cooking_in_Villages.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cookware_and_bakeware" title="Cookware and bakeware">Pots</a> being heated to cook <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Dwelling" title="Dwelling">dwelling</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3673" data-file-width="5667" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg/220px-Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg/330px-Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg/440px-Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Food_creation_-_Making_of_Akyeke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Food creation - Making of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akyeke" title="Akyeke">Akyeke</a> in Ghana</div></div></div>
<p><b>Cooking</b>, <b>cookery</b>, or <b>culinary arts</b> is the art, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and craft of using <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> to <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_food_preparation" title="Outline of food preparation">prepare food</a> for consumption. Cooking techniques and ingredients vary widely, from grilling food over an open <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a> to using <a href="/wiki/Electric_stove" title="Electric stove">electric stoves</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baking</a> in various types of <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">ovens</a>, reflecting local conditions.
</p><p>Types of cooking also depend on the skill levels and training of the <a href="/wiki/Cook_(profession)" title="Cook (profession)">cooks</a>. Cooking is done both by people in their own dwellings and by professional cooks and <a href="/wiki/Chef" title="Chef">chefs</a> in <a href="/wiki/Restaurant" title="Restaurant">restaurants</a> and other food establishments.
</p><p>Preparing food with heat or fire is an activity unique to <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>. Archeological evidence of cooking fires from at least 300,000 years ago exists, but some estimate that humans started cooking up to 2 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The expansion of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transportation</a> between <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a> in different regions offered cooks many new ingredients. New inventions and technologies, such as the invention of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> for holding and <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, expanded cooking techniques. Some modern cooks apply advanced scientific techniques to food preparation to further enhance the <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavor</a> of the dish served.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">Phylogenetic analysis</a> suggests that early hominins may have adopted cooking 1.8 million to 2.3 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-4">[4]</a></sup> Re-analysis of burnt bone fragments and plant ashes from the <a href="/wiki/Wonderwerk_Cave" title="Wonderwerk Cave">Wonderwerk Cave</a> in South Africa has provided evidence supporting <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">control of fire by early humans</a> by 1 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2012_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2012-5">[5]</a></sup> In his seminal work <a href="/wiki/Catching_Fire:_How_Cooking_Made_Us_Human" title="Catching Fire: How Cooking Made Us Human">Catching Fire: How Cooking Made Us Human</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wrangham" title="Richard Wrangham">Richard Wrangham</a> suggested that evolution of bipedalism and a large cranial capacity meant that early <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> regularly cooked food <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollard_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollard-7">[7]</a></sup> However, unequivocal evidence in the archaeological record for the controlled use of fire begins at 400,000 BCE, long after <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luke_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luke-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovermagazine.com_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovermagazine.com-9">[9]</a></sup> Archaeological evidence from 300,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> in the form of ancient hearths, <a href="/wiki/Earth_oven" title="Earth oven">earth ovens</a>, burnt animal bones, and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a>, are found across Europe and the Middle East. <a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">Anthropologists</a> think that widespread cooking fires began about 250,000 years ago when <a href="/wiki/Hearth" title="Hearth">hearths</a> first appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recently, the earliest hearths have been reported to be at least 790,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI-2016cook_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI-2016cook-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_(II)_005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2435" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg/170px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg/255px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg/340px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_%28II%29_005.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet_(II)_005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical oven <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baking</a>, in a painting by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Millet" title="Jean-François Millet">Jean-François Millet</a>, 1854</div></div></div>
<p>Communication between the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a> influenced the history of cooking. The movement of foods across the Atlantic from the New World, such as <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bell_pepper" title="Bell pepper">bell pepper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili pepper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin" title="Pumpkin">pumpkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cashew" title="Cashew">cashew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blueberry" title="Blueberry">blueberry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helianthus" title="Helianthus">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gourd" title="Gourd">gourds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squash</a>, had a profound effect on Old World cooking. The movement of foods across the Atlantic from the Old World, such as <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pear" title="Pear">pears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_bean" title="Green bean">green beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">mustard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carrot" title="Carrot">carrots</a>, similarly changed New World cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries, food was a classic marker of identity in Europe. In the nineteenth-century "Age of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">Nationalism</a>" cuisine became a defining symbol of national identity.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> brought mass-production, mass-marketing, and standardization of food. Factories processed, preserved, canned, and packaged a wide variety of foods, and processed cereals quickly became a defining feature of the American breakfast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Frozen_food" title="Frozen food">freezing methods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cafeteria" title="Cafeteria">cafeterias</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_food_restaurant" title="Fast food restaurant">fast food restaurants</a> emerged.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingredients">Ingredients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ingredients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Most ingredients in cooking are derived from <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">living organisms</a>. Vegetables, fruits, grains and nuts as well as herbs and spices come from plants, while meat, eggs, and dairy products come from animals. Mushrooms and the yeast used in baking are kinds of <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>. Cooks also use <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>. Cooks can also use <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">spirits</a>.
</p><p>Naturally occurring ingredients contain various amounts of molecules called <i><a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">proteins</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a></i>. They also contain water and minerals. Cooking involves a manipulation of the chemical properties of these molecules.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrate</a></div>
<p>Carbohydrates include the common sugar, <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (table sugar), a <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharide</a>, and such simple sugars as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (made by enzymatic splitting of sucrose) and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> (from fruit), and <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starches</a> from sources such as cereal flour, rice, <a href="/wiki/Arrowroot" title="Arrowroot">arrowroot</a> and potato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The interaction of heat and carbohydrate is complex. <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">Long-chain sugars</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> tend to break down into more digestible <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">simpler sugars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> If the sugars are heated so that all water of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystallisation" title="Crystallisation">crystallisation</a> is driven off, <a href="/wiki/Caramelization" title="Caramelization">caramelization</a> starts, with the sugar undergoing thermal decomposition with the formation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, and other breakdown products producing <a href="/wiki/Caramel" title="Caramel">caramel</a>. Similarly, the heating of sugars and proteins causes the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a>, a basic flavor-enhancing technique.
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Emulsion" title="Emulsion">emulsion</a> of starch with fat or water can, when gently heated, provide thickening to the dish being cooked. In <a href="/wiki/European_cuisine" title="European cuisine">European</a> cooking, a mixture of butter and flour called a <a href="/wiki/Roux" title="Roux">roux</a> is used to thicken liquids to make stews or sauces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In Asian cooking, a similar effect is obtained from a mixture of rice or <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a> and water. These techniques rely on the properties of starches to create simpler mucilaginous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccharide" title="Saccharide">saccharides</a> during cooking, which causes the familiar thickening of <a href="/wiki/Sauce" title="Sauce">sauces</a>. This thickening will break down, however, under additional heat.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fats">Fats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">Fat</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frying_doughnuts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Frying_doughnuts.jpg/220px-Frying_doughnuts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Frying_doughnuts.jpg/330px-Frying_doughnuts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Frying_doughnuts.jpg/440px-Frying_doughnuts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frying_doughnuts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Doughnut" title="Doughnut">Doughnuts</a> frying in oil</div></div></div>
<p>Types of fat include <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a>, animal products such as butter and <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">lard</a>, as well as fats from grains, including <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> oils. Fats are used in a number of ways in cooking and baking. To prepare <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stir_fry" title="Stir fry">stir fries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grilled_cheese" title="Grilled cheese">grilled cheese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancakes</a>, the pan or griddle is often coated with fat or oil. Fats are also used as an ingredient in baked goods such as cookies, cakes and pies. Fats can reach temperatures higher than the boiling point of water, and are often used to conduct high heat to other ingredients, such as in frying, deep frying or sautéing. Fats are used to add flavor to food (e.g., butter or bacon fat), prevent food from sticking to pans and create a desirable texture.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteins">Proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">Protein (nutrient)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_denaturation" title="Protein denaturation">Protein denaturation</a></div>
<p>Edible animal material, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a>, milk, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">egg whites</a>, contains substantial amounts of protein. Almost all vegetable matter (in particular <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a>) also includes proteins, although generally in smaller amounts. Mushrooms have high protein content. Any of these may be sources of <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>. When <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> are heated they become <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denatured</a> (unfolded) and change texture. In many cases, this causes the structure of the material to become softer or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friable" title="Friable">friable</a> – meat becomes <i>cooked</i> and is more friable and less flexible. In some cases, proteins can form more rigid structures, such as the coagulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albumen" title="Albumen">albumen</a> in egg whites. The formation of a relatively rigid but flexible matrix from egg white provides an important component in baking cakes, and also underpins many desserts based on <a href="/wiki/Meringue" title="Meringue">meringue</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuisson_des_pates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/220px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/330px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/440px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuisson_des_pates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water is often used to cook foods such as <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodles</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a></div>
<p>Cooking often involves water and water-based liquids. These can be added in order to immerse the substances being cooked (this is typically done with water, <a href="/wiki/Stock_(food)" title="Stock (food)">stock</a> or wine). Alternatively, the foods themselves can release water. A favorite method of adding flavor to dishes is to save the liquid for use in other recipes.  Liquids are so important to cooking that the name of the cooking method used is often based on how the liquid is combined with the food, as in <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">steaming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simmering" title="Simmering">simmering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Braising" title="Braising">braising</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blanching_(cooking)" title="Blanching (cooking)">blanching</a>. Heating liquid in an open container results in rapidly increased <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Concentrate" title="Concentrate">concentrates</a> the remaining <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavor</a> and ingredients – this is a critical component of both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stewing" title="Stewing">stewing</a> and sauce making.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitamins_and_minerals">Vitamins and minerals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vitamins and minerals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:November_15,_2012,_The_Right_Cut_(8223639251).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg/220px-November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg/330px-November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg/440px-November_15%2C_2012%2C_The_Right_Cut_%288223639251%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:November_15,_2012,_The_Right_Cut_(8223639251).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vegetables contain important vitamins and minerals</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">Vitamin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">Mineral (nutrient)</a></div>
<p>Vitamins and minerals are required for normal <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> but which the body cannot manufacture itself and which must therefore come from external sources. Vitamins come from several sources including fresh fruit and vegetables (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a>), carrots, <a href="/wiki/Liver_(food)" title="Liver (food)">liver</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">Vitamin A</a>), cereal bran, bread, liver (B vitamins), fish liver oil (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>) and fresh green vegetables (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">Vitamin K</a>). Many minerals are also essential in small quantities including iron, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>; and in very small quantities copper, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>. The micronutrients, minerals, and vitamins<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> in fruit and vegetables may be destroyed or eluted by cooking. Vitamin C is especially prone to oxidation during cooking and may be completely destroyed by protracted cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2013)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> The bioavailability of some vitamins such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiamin" title="Thiamin">thiamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin_(nutrient)" title="Niacin (nutrient)">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">folate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> are increased with cooking by being freed from the food microstructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Blanching or steaming vegetables is a way of minimizing vitamin and mineral loss in cooking.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cooking_techniques" title="List of cooking techniques">List of cooking techniques</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are very many methods of cooking, most of which have been known since antiquity. These include baking, roasting, frying, grilling, barbecuing, smoking, boiling, steaming and braising. A more recent innovation is microwaving. Various methods use differing levels of heat and moisture and vary in cooking time. The method chosen greatly affects the end result because some foods are more appropriate to some methods than others. Some major hot cooking techniques include:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg/220px-Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg/330px-Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg/440px-Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sautee_onions_and_peppers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cook sautees onions and <a href="/wiki/Bell_pepper" title="Bell pepper">green peppers</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">skillet</a>.</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Roasting</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">Roasting</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbecuing" title="Barbecuing">Barbecuing</a> – <a href="/wiki/Grilling" title="Grilling">Grilling</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broiling" title="Broiling">Broiling</a> – <a href="/wiki/Rotisserie" title="Rotisserie">Rotisserie</a> – <a href="/wiki/Searing" title="Searing">Searing</a></dd>
<dt>Baking</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">Baking</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_Baking" title="Blind Baking">Baking Blind</a> – Flashbaking</dd>
<dt>Boiling</dt>
<dd><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boiling_in_cooking" title="Boiling in cooking">Boiling</a> – <a href="/wiki/Blanching_(cooking)" title="Blanching (cooking)">Blanching</a> – <a href="/wiki/Braising" title="Braising">Braising</a> – <a href="/wiki/Coddling" title="Coddling">Coddling</a> – <a href="/wiki/Double_steaming" title="Double steaming">Double steaming</a> – <a href="/wiki/Infusion" title="Infusion">Infusion</a> – <a href="/wiki/Poaching_(cooking)" title="Poaching (cooking)">Poaching</a> – <a href="/wiki/Pressure_cooking" title="Pressure cooking">Pressure cooking</a> – <a href="/wiki/Simmering" title="Simmering">Simmering</a> – <a href="/wiki/Smothering_(food)" title="Smothering (food)">Smothering</a> – <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">Steaming</a> – <a href="/wiki/Steeping" title="Steeping">Steeping</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stewing" title="Stewing">Stewing</a> – <a href="/wiki/Stone_boiling" title="Stone boiling">Stone boiling</a> –  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacuum_flask_cooking" title="Vacuum flask cooking">Vacuum flask cooking</a></dd>
<dt>Frying</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Frying" title="Frying">Fry</a> – <a href="/wiki/Air_fryer" title="Air fryer">Air frying</a> — <a href="/wiki/Deep_frying" title="Deep frying">Deep frying</a> – <a href="/wiki/Gentle_frying" title="Gentle frying">Gentle frying</a> - <a href="/wiki/Hot_salt_frying" title="Hot salt frying">Hot salt frying</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_sand_frying" title="Hot sand frying">Hot sand frying</a> – <a href="/wiki/Pan_frying" title="Pan frying">Pan frying</a> – <a href="/wiki/Pressure_frying" title="Pressure frying">Pressure frying</a> – <a href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9ing" title="Sautéing">Sautéing</a>  – <a href="/wiki/Shallow_frying" title="Shallow frying">Shallow frying</a> – <a href="/wiki/Stir_frying" title="Stir frying">Stir frying</a> — <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_fryer" title="Vacuum fryer">Vacuum frying</a></dd>
<dt>Steaming</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">Steaming</a> works by boiling water continuously, causing it to vaporise into steam; the steam then carries heat to the nearby food, thus cooking the food.  By many it is considered a healthy form of cooking, holding nutrients within the vegetable or meat being cooked.</dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/En_papillote" title="En papillote">En papillote</a> – The food is put into a pouch and then baked, allowing its own moisture to steam the food.</dd>
<dt>Smoking</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Smoking_(cooking)" title="Smoking (cooking)">Smoking</a> is the process of flavoring, cooking, or preserving food by exposing it to smoke from burning or smoldering material, most often wood.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_safety">Health and safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Health and safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indoor_air_pollution">Indoor air pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Indoor air pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Household_air_pollution" title="Household air pollution">Household air pollution</a></div>
<p>As of 2021, over 2.6 billion people cook using open fires or inefficient stoves using <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> as fuel. These cooking practices use fuels and technologies that produce high levels of household air pollution, causing 3.8 million premature deaths annually. Of these deaths, 27% are from <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, 27% from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ischaemic_heart_disease" title="Ischaemic heart disease">ischaemic heart disease</a>, 20% from <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a>, 18% from <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, and 8% from <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>. Women and young children are disproportionately affected, since they spend the most time near the hearth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-airpollution_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-airpollution-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_while_cooking">Security while cooking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Security while cooking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hazards while cooking can include
</p>
<ul><li>Unseen slippery surfaces (such as from oil stains or water droplets)</li>
<li>Cuts (1 percent of injuries in United States related to knives, ended in hospital admissions. In overall 400 000 injuries from knives are recorded in the US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li>Burns or fires</li></ul>
<p>To prevent those injuries there are protections such as cooking clothing, anti-slip shoes, fire extinguisher and more.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_safety">Food safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Food safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a></div>
<p>Cooking can prevent many <a href="/wiki/Foodborne_illness" title="Foodborne illness">foodborne illnesses</a> that would otherwise occur if the food is eaten raw. When heat is used in the preparation of food, it can kill or inactivate harmful organisms, such as bacteria and viruses, as well as various parasites such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapeworm" title="Tapeworm">tapeworms</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii" title="Toxoplasma gondii">Toxoplasma gondii</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">Food poisoning</a> and other illness from uncooked or poorly prepared food may be caused by bacteria such as <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli_O157:H7" title="Escherichia coli O157:H7">pathogenic strains</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonella_typhimurium" title="Salmonella typhimurium">Salmonella typhimurium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">Campylobacter</a></i>, viruses such as <a href="/wiki/Norovirus" title="Norovirus">noroviruses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Entamoeba_histolytica" title="Entamoeba histolytica">Entamoeba histolytica</a></i>. Bacteria, viruses and parasites may be introduced through salad, meat that is uncooked or done <a href="/wiki/Doneness" title="Doneness">rare</a>, and unboiled water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilizing</a> effect of cooking depends on temperature, cooking time, and technique used. Some <a href="/wiki/Food_spoilage" title="Food spoilage">food spoilage</a> bacteria such as <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_botulinum" title="Clostridium botulinum">Clostridium botulinum</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_cereus" title="Bacillus cereus">Bacillus cereus</a></i> can form spores that survive boiling, which then <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germinate</a> and regrow after the food has cooled. This makes it unsafe to reheat cooked food more than once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cooking increases the digestibility of many foods which are inedible or poisonous when raw. For example, raw cereal grains are hard to digest, while <a href="/wiki/Kidney_bean" title="Kidney bean">kidney beans</a> are toxic when raw or improperly cooked due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Phytohaemagglutinin" title="Phytohaemagglutinin">phytohaemagglutinin</a>, which is inactivated by cooking for at least ten minutes at 100 °C (212 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food safety depends on the safe preparation, handling, and storage of food. Food spoilage bacteria proliferate in the "<a href="/wiki/Danger_zone_(food_safety)" title="Danger zone (food safety)">Danger zone</a>" temperature range from 40 to 140 °F (4 to 60 °C), food therefore should not be stored in this temperature range. <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">Washing of hands</a> and surfaces, especially when handling different meats, and keeping raw food separate from cooked food to avoid cross-contamination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> are good practices in food preparation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Foods prepared on plastic cutting boards may be less likely to harbor bacteria than wooden ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReluctantGourmet_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReluctantGourmet-29">[29]</a></sup> Washing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disinfection" title="Disinfection">disinfecting</a> cutting boards, especially after use with raw meat, poultry, or seafood, reduces the risk of contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReluctantGourmet_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReluctantGourmet-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_nutritional_content_of_food">Effects on nutritional content of food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects on nutritional content of food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">Raw foodism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rawtomatopasta1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1130" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Rawtomatopasta1.jpg/220px-Rawtomatopasta1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Rawtomatopasta1.jpg/330px-Rawtomatopasta1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Rawtomatopasta1.jpg/440px-Rawtomatopasta1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rawtomatopasta1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A raw <a href="/wiki/Tomato_sauce" title="Tomato sauce">tomato sauce</a> with <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celery" title="Celery">celery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnuts</a> on <a href="/wiki/Zucchini" title="Zucchini">zucchini</a> noodles.</div></div></div>
<p>Proponents of <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">raw foodism</a> argue that cooking food increases the risk of some of the detrimental effects on food or health. They point out that during cooking of vegetables and fruit containing <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, the vitamin elutes into the cooking water and becomes degraded through oxidation. Peeling vegetables can also substantially reduce the vitamin C content, especially in the case of potatoes where most vitamin C is in the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> However, research has shown that in the specific case of <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> a greater proportion is absorbed from cooked vegetables than from raw vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>German research in 2003 showed significant benefits in reducing breast cancer risk when large amounts of raw vegetable matter are included in the diet. The authors attribute some of this effect to heat-labile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytonutrient" title="Phytonutrient">phytonutrients</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sulforaphane" title="Sulforaphane">Sulforaphane</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Glucosinolate" title="Glucosinolate">glucosinolate</a> breakdown product, which may be found in vegetables such as <a href="/wiki/Broccoli" title="Broccoli">broccoli</a>, has been shown to be protective against <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>; however, much of it is destroyed when the vegetable is boiled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>  Although there has been some basic research on how <a href="/wiki/Sulforaphane" title="Sulforaphane">sulforaphane</a> might exert beneficial effects in vivo, there is no high-quality evidence for its efficacy against human diseases.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> has studied retention data for 16 vitamins, 8 minerals, and alcohol for approximately 290 foods for various cooking methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carcinogens">Carcinogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Carcinogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBQ_Food.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1728" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BBQ_Food.jpg/220px-BBQ_Food.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BBQ_Food.jpg/330px-BBQ_Food.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BBQ_Food.jpg/440px-BBQ_Food.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBQ_Food.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" title="Chicken (food)">Chicken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a>-wrapped <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> cooking in a <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecue</a> smoker. Barbecuing and smoking generate carcinogens.</div></div></div>
<p>In a human epidemiological analysis by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Doll" title="Richard Doll">Richard Doll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Peto" title="Richard Peto">Richard Peto</a> in 1981, diet was estimated to cause a large percentage of cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Studies suggest that around 32% of cancer deaths may be avoidable by changes to the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Some of these cancers may be caused by carcinogens in food generated during the cooking process, although it is often difficult to identify the specific components in diet that serve to increase cancer risk. Many foods, such as <a href="/wiki/Beefsteak" title="Beefsteak">beefsteak</a> and broccoli, contain low concentrations of both <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anticarcinogen" title="Anticarcinogen">anticarcinogens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies published since 1990 indicate that cooking meat at high temperature creates <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> (HCAs), which are thought to increase cancer risk in humans. Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/National_Cancer_Institute" title="National Cancer Institute">National Cancer Institute</a> found that human subjects who ate beef rare or medium-rare had less than one third the risk of stomach cancer than those who ate beef medium-well or well-done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amines_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-amines-38">[38]</a></sup> While avoiding meat or eating meat raw may be the only ways to avoid HCAs in meat fully, the National Cancer Institute states that cooking meat below 212 °F (100 °C) creates "negligible amounts" of HCAs. Also, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaving" title="Microwaving">microwaving</a> meat before cooking may reduce HCAs by 90% by reducing the time needed for the meat to be cooked at high heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amines_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-amines-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">Nitrosamines</a> are found in some food, and may be produced by some cooking processes from proteins or from nitrites used as food preservatives; cured meat such as bacon has been found to be carcinogenic, with links to colon cancer. <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Ascorbate</a>, which is added to cured meat, however, reduces nitrosamine formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baking, grilling or broiling food, especially starchy foods, until a toasted crust is formed generates significant concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Acrylamide" title="Acrylamide">acrylamide</a>. This discovery in 2002 led to international health concerns. Subsequent research has however found that it is not likely that the acrylamides in burnt or well-cooked food cause cancer in humans; <a href="/wiki/Cancer_Research_UK" title="Cancer Research UK">Cancer Research UK</a> categorizes the idea that burnt food causes cancer as a "myth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cruk_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-cruk-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_health_issues">Other health issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other health issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cooking dairy products may reduce a protective effect against colon cancer. Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a> suggest that ingesting uncooked or unpasteurized <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy products</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Raw_milk" title="Raw milk">Raw milk</a>) may reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Mice and rats fed uncooked sucrose, casein, and beef tallow had one-third to one-fifth the incidence of <a href="/wiki/Adenoma" title="Adenoma">microadenomas</a> as the mice and rats fed the same ingredients cooked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> This claim, however, is contentious. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration_(United_States)" title="Food and Drug Administration (United States)">Food and Drug Administration of the United States</a>, health benefits claimed by raw milk advocates do not exist. "The small quantities of antibodies in milk are not absorbed in the human intestinal tract," says Barbara Ingham, PhD, associate professor and extension food scientist at the University of Wisconsin-Madison. "There is no scientific evidence that raw milk contains an anti-arthritis factor or that it enhances resistance to other diseases."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Heating sugars with proteins or fats can produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_glycation_end_product" title="Advanced glycation end product">advanced glycation end products</a> ("glycotoxins").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kosch_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-kosch-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deep fried food in restaurants may contain high level of <a href="/wiki/Trans_fat" title="Trans fat">trans fat</a>, which is known to increase levels of <a href="/wiki/Low-density_lipoprotein" title="Low-density lipoprotein">low-density lipoprotein</a> that in turn may increase risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_disease" title="Coronary disease">heart diseases</a> and other conditions. However, many fast food chains have now switched to trans-fat-free alternatives for deep-frying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDs_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDs-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_aspects">Scientific aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Scientific aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_gastronomy" title="Molecular gastronomy">Molecular gastronomy</a></div>
<p>The scientific study of cooking has become known as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_gastronomy" title="Molecular gastronomy">molecular gastronomy</a>. This is a subdiscipline of <a href="/wiki/Food_science" title="Food science">food science</a> concerning the physical and chemical transformations that occur during cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important contributions have been made by scientists, chefs and authors such as <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_This" title="Hervé This">Hervé This</a> (chemist), <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Kurti" title="Nicholas Kurti">Nicholas Kurti</a> (physicist), <a href="/wiki/Peter_Barham" title="Peter Barham">Peter Barham</a> (physicist), <a href="/wiki/Harold_McGee" title="Harold McGee">Harold McGee</a> (author), <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Corriher" title="Shirley Corriher">Shirley Corriher</a> (biochemist, author),  <a href="/wiki/Robert_Wolke" title="Robert Wolke">Robert Wolke</a> (chemist, author.) It is different for the application of scientific knowledge to cooking, that is "molecular cooking"( (for the technique) or "molecular cuisine" (for a culinary style), for which chefs such as Raymond Blanc, Philippe and Christian Conticini, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferran_Adria" title="Ferran Adria">Ferran Adria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heston_Blumenthal" title="Heston Blumenthal">Heston Blumenthal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gagnaire" title="Pierre Gagnaire">Pierre Gagnaire</a> (chef).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemical processes central to cooking include hydrolysis (in particular beta elimination of pectins, during the thermal treatment of plant tissues), pyrolysis, glycation reactions wrongly named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maillard_reactions" title="Maillard reactions">Maillard reactions</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cooking foods with heat depends on many factors — the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a> of an object, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, and perhaps most significantly the difference in temperature between the two objects. <a href="/wiki/Thermal_diffusivity" title="Thermal diffusivity">Thermal diffusivity</a> is the combination of specific heat, conductivity and <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> that determines how long it will take for the food to reach a certain temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Home-cooking_and_commercial_cooking">Home-cooking and commercial cooking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cooking&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Home-cooking and commercial cooking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:K%C3%BCche.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/K%C3%BCche.JPG/220px-K%C3%BCche.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/K%C3%BCche.JPG/330px-K%C3%BCche.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/K%C3%BCche.JPG/440px-K%C3%BCche.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:K%C3%BCche.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A restaurant kitchen in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, Germany (Haxnbauer restaurant)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg/220px-Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg/330px-Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg/440px-Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Food_creation_in_black_and_white.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman cooking in a big pot</div></div></div>
<p>Home cooking has traditionally been a process carried out informally in a home or around a <a href="/wiki/Communal_oven" title="Communal oven">communal fire</a>, and can be enjoyed by all members of the family, although in many cultures women bear primary responsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Cooking is also often carried out outside of personal quarters, for example at restaurants, or schools. <a href="/wiki/Bakery" title="Bakery">Bakeries</a> were one of the earliest forms of cooking outside the home, and bakeries in the past often offered the cooking of pots of food provided by their customers as an additional service. In the present day, factory food preparation has become common, with many "ready-to-eat" as well as "ready-to-cook" foods being prepared and cooked in factories and home cooks using a mixture of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/from_scratch" title="wikt:from scratch">scratch made</a>, and factory made foods together to make a <a href="/wiki/Meal" title="Meal">meal</a>. The nutritional value of including more commercially prepared foods has been found to be inferior to home-made foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Home-cooked meals tend to be healthier with fewer calories, and less <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol_embolism" title="Cholesterol embolism">cholesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> on a per calorie basis while providing more <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The ingredients are also directly sourced, so there is control over authenticity, taste, and nutritional value. The superior nutritional quality of home-cooking could therefore play a role in preventing <a href="/wiki/Chronic_condition" title="Chronic condition">chronic disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Cohort studies following the elderly over 10 years show that adults who cook their own meals have significantly lower mortality, even when controlling for confounding variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Home-cooking" may be associated with <a href="/wiki/Comfort_food" title="Comfort food">comfort food</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and some commercially produced foods and restaurant meals are presented through advertising or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Packaging" title="Packaging">packaging</a> as having been "home-cooked", regardless of their actual origin. This trend began in the 1920s and is attributed to people in urban areas of the U.S. wanting homestyle food even though their schedules and smaller kitchens made cooking harder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
